[3:3:66,107] 3810 - REGIONAL OFFICES AND REGIONAL ADVISORY COMMITTEES  

[4:0:66,127] Twelve Regional Offices and Regional Advisory Committees provide administrative support and assist with advocacy, training,  

[4:1:66,137] coordination, and implementation of State Plan objectives in Council Regions throughout California. These offices and advisory  

[4:2:66,148] committees provide information and data to the Council to assess regional needs and implementation of the State Plan and for  

[4:3:66,158] inclusion in reports to the federal government and the Legislature.  

[5:0:66,178] In addition to implementation of State Plan activities, Regional Office staff support the following activities to improve and  

[5:1:66,188] enhance the availability and quality of services for residents of state developmental centers and state-operated community  

[5:2:66,199] facilities:  

[6:0:65,216] • Advocacy services through volunteers recruited by Council staff for individuals who have no legally appointed representative  

[6:1:76,226] to assist them in making choices and decisions.  

[6:2:65,243] • Clients' rights advocacy services to ensure that laws, regulations, and policies pertaining to the rights of persons with  

[6:3:76,253] developmental disabilities are observed.  

[6:4:65,269] • Quality Assessments for individuals who receive community residential services and support.

[8:0:66,422] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[8:1:65,442] • COVID-19 Direct Response Expenditures— The Budget includes a total of $1.7 billion for various state departments for  

[8:2:76,452] direct response costs associated with responding to and mitigating the impacts of the COVID-19 Pandemic in 2021-22.  

[8:3:76,462] Specifically, the Budget includes $16.9 million General Fund for the Emergency Medical Services Authority for ambulance  

[8:4:76,472] transportation, medical personnel support, and infrastructure support.  

[9:0:65,489] • Emergency Capacity—The Budget includes $10.8 million General Fund in 2021-22 to   store and maintain critical emergency  

[9:1:76,499] equipment and medical supplies acquired during the pandemic, and staff resources to improve the on-boarding, deployment,  

[9:2:76,509] and training for emergency medical responders. Beginning in 2023-24, ongoing costs decrease to $9 million General Fund.  

[9:3:65,525] • Statewide Emergency Medical Services Data Solution— The Budget includes $10 million General Fund one-time for  

[9:4:76,535] planning and readiness activities to establish a statewide emergency services data infrastructure that strengthens real-time  

[9:5:76,546] information sharing and data analytics for the state and local government, emergency medical services providers, and health  

[9:6:76,556] care providers.  

[10:0:65,572] • Physician Orders for Life Sustaining Treatment (POLST)— The Budget includes $10   million General Fund in 2021-22 and  

[10:1:76,582] $750,000 ongoing for a POLST Registry.

[5:0:66,574] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,593] 3820 - EMERGENCY MEDICAL SERVICES AUTHORITY  

[5:2:66,611] Disaster Medical Services Division  

[6:0:66,628] The Disaster Medical Services Division coordinates California's medical response to major disasters by carrying out the  

[6:1:66,639] Authority's mandate to provide medical resources to local governments in support of their disaster response efforts. The  

[6:2:66,649] Division coordinates with the Governor's Office of Emergency Services, the Office of Homeland Security, the California National  

[6:3:66,659] Guard, the California Department of Public Health, and other local, state, and federal agencies, private sector hospitals,  

[6:4:66,669] ambulance companies, and medical supply vendors, to promote and improve disaster preparedness and emergency medical  

[6:5:66,679] response in California.  

[6:6:66,697] Emergency Medical Services Personnel Division

[0:0:66,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — HHS   6 

[1:0:419,13] HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES 

[2:0:141,32] 4120   Emergency Medical Services Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The Emergency Medical Services Personnel Division is responsible for licensing and enforcement actions of all active  

[4:0:66,79] paramedics throughout the state. The Division develops and implements regulations that set training, certification and licensure  

[4:1:66,89] standards, and the scope of practice for various emergency medical services personnel. The Division also sets standards for,  

[4:2:66,99] and approves, training programs in: pediatric first aid, CPR,   preventive health practices for child day care providers, and school  

[4:3:66,109] bus drivers. It also develops standards for emergency medical dispatcher training, pre-arrival emergency care instructions, and  

[4:4:66,120] the epinephrine auto-injector training program.  

[4:5:66,137] Emergency Medical Services Systems Division  

[5:0:66,155] The Emergency Medical Services Systems Division is responsible for developing and implementing emergency medical  

[5:1:66,165] services systems throughout California, including the support of local Health Information Exchange projects that will allow the  

[5:2:66,175] state to collect more meaningful data so that emergency medical services providers can deliver better patient care. The  

[5:3:66,186] Division also oversees system development and implementation by the local Emergency Medical Services agencies, the  

[5:4:66,196] statewide trauma system, and emergency medical dispatcher and communication standards.   Additionally, it   establishes  

[5:5:66,206] regulations and guidelines for local agencies; reviews and approves local plans to ensure they meet the minimum state  

[5:6:66,216] standards; coordinates injury and illness prevention activities with the California Department of Public Health and the Office of  

[5:7:66,226] Traffic Safety; manages the state's emergency medical services data and quality improvement process; conducts Ambulance  

[5:8:66,237] Exclusive Operating Area evaluations; oversees the operation of California's Poison Control System; and manages the  

[5:9:66,247] Emergency Medical Services for Children programs.

[5:0:66,243] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,262] 3831 - HEALTH CARE QUALITY AND AFFORDABILITY  

[6:0:66,280] The   Health Care Quality and Affordability Program   provides a comprehensive understanding of health care cost trends and  

[6:1:66,290] drivers of spending and   implements strategies for controlling costs, while maintaining quality care and   promoting savings for  

[6:2:66,300] consumers. 

[6:3:66,318] 3835 - HEALTH CARE WORKFORCE  

[7:0:66,338] The Health Care Workforce Program, through the Health Care Workforce Development Division improves access to medical,  

[7:1:66,349] mental, and dental health care providers in underserved areas throughout California. The Program conducts research to  

[7:2:66,359] identify areas of unmet need and administers grants that provide financial incentives to individuals and institutions to increase  

[7:3:66,369] the number of providers in those areas. The Program promotes health care workforce diversity and cultural competency. It  

[7:4:66,379] includes the following programs:  

[7:5:65,397] • Song-Brown Health Care Workforce Training Program  

[7:6:65,413] • Mental Health Services Act Workforce Education and Training Program  

[7:7:65,429] • Health Workforce Research and Data Center  

[7:8:65,445] • Health Professions Career Opportunity Training Program  

[7:9:65,462] • Health Workforce Pilot Projects Program  

[7:10:65,478] • California's Primary Care Office, which includes:  

[7:11:74,496] ◦ Shortage Designation Program  

[7:12:74,512] ◦ State Office of Rural Health 

[7:13:74,528] ◦ J-1 Visa Waiver Program  

[8:0:65,552] • Scholarship Programs  

[9:0:65,568] • Loan Repayment Programs, including the California State Loan Repayment Program, Steven M. Thompson Physician Corps  

[9:1:76,578] Loan Repayment Program and Licensed Mental Health Services Provider Education Program  

[9:2:65,594] • Children and Youth Behavioral Health Initiative Workforce Programs  

[9:3:66,612] 3840 - FACILITIES DEVELOPMENT  

[10:0:66,630] The Facilities Development Program safeguards public health, safety, and general welfare through regulation of the design and  

[10:1:66,640] construction of health care facilities, including compliance with seismic safety requirements, to ensure they are capable of  

[10:2:66,650] providing sustained services to the public.  

[10:3:66,668] 3845 - CAL-MORTGAGE LOAN INSURANCE  

[11:0:66,685] The Cal-Mortgage Program is modeled after federal home mortgage insurance programs and insures loans to public and  

[11:1:66,696] nonprofit health care facilities for construction, renovation, and expansion projects. The Program underwrites loans, monitors  

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:99,32] 4140   Office of Statewide Health Planning and Development - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] the Cal-Mortgage insured loan portfolio, and administers the Health Facility Construction Loan Insurance Fund. By facilitating  

[3:1:66,79] access to private capital at no cost to taxpayers, the Program has improved the delivery of health care throughout California.  

[3:2:66,97] 3855 - HEALTH CARE INFORMATION AND QUALITY ANALYSIS 

[4:0:66,114] The Health Care Information Program sets standards for, collects, and maintains financial and utilization data from  

[5:0:66,124] approximately 7,000 licensed health facilities in California, as well as comprehensive demographic, diagnostic, and treatment  

[5:1:66,135] data for all patients discharged from licensed hospitals, treated in emergency departments, or having had an ambulatory  

[5:2:66,145] surgery procedure in hospital surgical clinics. This information is used by health care policymakers, health care providers,  

[5:3:66,155] health planners, public and private sector health care purchasers, researchers, consumers, and the media. To further this  

[5:4:66,165] mission, the Health Care Information Program is implementing new health care cost transparency data programs to collect and  

[5:5:66,175] analyze prescription drug cost data, hospital supplier diversity data, and health care payment data to improve transparency,  

[5:6:66,186] inform policy decisions, reduce disparities, and reduce health care costs. 

[5:7:66,203] 3860 - ADMINISTRATION  

[6:0:66,224] Administration provides overall management, planning, policy development, legal, legislative, information technology support  

[6:1:66,234] and administrative services to HCAI.

[4:0:184,157] 4150   Department of Managed Health Care  

[4:1:66,178] The Department of Managed Health Care protects health care consumers   and ensures a stable health care delivery system in  

[4:2:66,188] California. The Department achieves this mission by:  

[4:3:65,206] • Licensing and regulating the full scope of managed care models, including all Health Maintenance Organizations (HMO) in  

[4:4:76,216] the state, Preferred Provider Organizations (PPO), Exclusive Provider Organizations (EPO), Point-of-Service (POS) products  

[4:5:76,226] and Medi-Cal managed care plans. The Department also licenses and conducts financial reviews of Medicare Advantage  

[4:6:76,236] and Part D plans and licenses and regulates specialized health plans including chiropractic, dental, vision, psychological  

[4:7:76,247] (behavioral health) and pharmacy.  

[4:8:65,263] • Enforcing the Knox-Keene Health Care Service Plan Act of 1975, as amended.  

[4:9:65,279] • Conducting medical surveys and financial examinations to verify that health plans are complying with the law and are  

[4:10:76,289] financially stable to serve their enrollees.  

[4:11:65,305] • Operating a 24-hour-a-day Help Center to resolve health care consumer complaints and administer the Independent Medical  

[4:12:76,316] Review program.  

[4:13:65,332] • Reviewing proposed health plan rate changes for sufficient justification and reasonableness.  

[4:14:65,348] • Monitoring the financial solvency of the medical groups with whom health plans contract to provide health benefits to their  

[4:15:76,358] enrollees.  

[5:0:65,374] • Convening the Financial Solvency Standards Board, comprised of people with expertise in the medical, financial and health  

[5:1:76,385] plan industries, to advise the Director on strategies to keep the managed care industry financially stable.

[5:0:66,334] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,353] 3870 - HEALTH PLAN PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,373] The Health Plan Program's primary objective is to regulate managed health care plans and assist consumers to resolve  

[6:1:66,384] disputes with health plans. The program's key functions to achieve this objective are to: license managed health care plans,  

[6:2:66,394] conduct routine and non-routine financial exams and medical surveys, review proposed premium rate increases, assist  

[6:3:66,404] consumers with health care issues and enable managed health care patients to receive the medical care and services to which  

[6:4:66,414] they are entitled, and operate a consumer services toll-free complaint line (1-888-466-2219).

[5:0:228,169] 4170   Department of Aging  

[6:0:66,191] The Department of Aging's mission is to promote the independence and well-being of older adults, adults with disabilities, and  

[6:1:66,201] families through:  

[7:0:65,219] • Access to information and services to improve the quality of their lives,  

[7:1:65,235] • Opportunities for community involvement,  

[8:0:65,251] • Support to family members providing care, and  

[8:1:65,267] • Collaboration with other state and local agencies.  

[9:0:66,285] As the federally designated State Unit on Aging, the Department administers federal Older Americans Act programs that  

[9:1:66,295] provide a wide variety of community-based supportive services and administers the Health Insurance Counseling and  

[10:0:66,305] Advocacy Program. The Department also administers two Medi-Cal programs: it contracts directly with agencies that operate  

[10:1:66,316] the Multipurpose Senior Services Program (MSSP) and provides oversight for the MSSP waiver, and certifies Community-

[10:2:66,326] Based Adult Services centers for participation in Medicaid.  

[11:0:66,346] The Department administers most of these programs through contracts with the state's 33 local Area Agencies on Aging. At the  

[11:1:66,356] local level, Area Agencies on Aging contract for and coordinate this array of community-based services to older adults, adults  

[11:2:66,367] with disabilities, family caregivers, and residents of long-term care facilities.

[13:0:66,320] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[14:0:65,339] • Older Adult Recovery and Resiliency—The Budget includes $106 million General Fund, available over three years, to  

[14:1:76,349] strengthen older adults’ recovery and resilience from isolation and health impacts caused by the pandemic. This investment  

[14:2:76,360] will increase service levels of existing programs based on local need including: Senior Nutrition, Senior Legal Aid, Home  

[14:3:76,370] Modifications and Fall Prevention, Behavioral Health Line, Senior Digital Connections, Family Caregiver Support, Senior  

[14:4:76,380] Employment Opportunities, Elder Abuse Prevention, and Aging and Disability Resource Connection.  

[15:0:65,396] • CalFresh Expansion Older Adult Outreach—The Budget includes $2 million ($1.1 million General Fund) ongoing for the  

[15:1:76,406] Department of Aging to continue CalFresh Expansion outreach efforts to older adults.  

[16:0:65,423] • Master Plan for Aging Implementation—The Budget includes $3.3 million General Fund ongoing to provide the Department  

[16:1:76,433] of Aging policy, project management, and information technology leadership necessary to implement the Master Plan for  

[16:2:76,443] Aging.  

[17:0:65,459] • Department of Aging Suspensions—The Budget eliminates suspensions for Aging and Disability Resource Connections and  

[17:1:76,469] Senior Nutrition resulting in a cost of $13.8 million General Fund in 2022-23 and $ 5 million General Fund ongoing.  

[18:0:65,486] • Long-Term Care Patient Representative Program—The Budget includes $2.5 million General Fund in 2021-22 and $4 million  

[18:1:76,496] General Fund ongoing to provide public patient representatives to nursing facility residents who lack capacity to make their  

[18:2:76,506] own health care decisions and who do not have a family member or friend who can act as a patient representative.  

[19:0:65,522] • Senior Nutrition—The Budget includes $14.3 million General Fund in 2021-22 and $35 million General Fund ongoing for  

[19:1:76,532] additional funding for increased meals and $40 million General Fund, one-time, in 2021-22 for capacity building for Senior  

[19:2:76,543] Nutrition programs.  

[20:0:65,559] • Area Agencies on Aging (AAA)—The Budget includes $3.3 million General Fund ongoing for baseline funding increases for

[8:0:66,618] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:1:66,637] 3890 - NUTRITION  

[9:0:66,658] The Nutrition Program provides nutritionally-balanced meals, nutrition education, and nutrition counseling to individuals 60  

[9:1:66,668] years of age or older. In addition to promoting better health through improved nutrition, the program focuses on reducing the  

[9:2:66,678] isolation of the elderly and providing a link to other social and supportive services such as transportation, information and  

[9:3:66,688] assistance, employment, and education.  

[10:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:193,32] 4170   Department of Aging - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] 3895 - SENIOR COMMUNITY EMPLOYMENT SERVICE  

[4:0:66,89] The federal Senior Community Service Employment Program, Title V of the Older Americans Act, provides part-time subsidized  

[4:1:66,99] training and employment in community service agencies for low-income persons 55 years of age and older. The program also  

[4:2:66,109] promotes transition to unsubsidized employment.  

[4:3:66,127] 3900 - SUPPORTIVE SERVICES  

[5:0:66,145] This program provides supportive services in the community to adults 60 years of age or older, their family caregivers,  

[5:1:66,155] grandparents caring for grandchildren, and residents of long-term care facilities. Services include information and assistance,  

[5:2:66,165] legal assistance, transportation, respite in-home support, senior center activities, elder abuse prevention, and the Long-Term  

[5:3:66,175] Care Ombudsman. Older Americans Act Titles III and VII fund these services to enable individuals to access the support  

[5:4:66,186] necessary for them to remain independent in their communities, continue in their caregiving role, and/or receive long-term care  

[5:5:66,196] services appropriate to their needs.  

[5:6:66,214] 3905 - COMMUNITY-BASED PROGRAMS AND PROJECTS  

[6:0:66,231] This program includes the community-based Health Insurance Counseling and Advocacy Program (HICAP). HICAP provides  

[6:1:66,241] personalized counseling, community education, and outreach events for Medicare beneficiaries. HICAP is the primary local  

[6:2:66,252] source for accurate and objective information and assistance with Medicare benefits, prescription drug plans, and health plans.  

[6:3:66,269] 3910 - MEDI-CAL PROGRAMS  

[7:0:66,287] This program includes oversight of the Multipurpose Senior Services Program (MSSP) and Community-Based Adult Services  

[7:1:66,297] (CBAS) program. CBAS is a community-based day health program that provides services to adults 18 years of age or over who  

[7:2:66,307] are at risk of needing institutional care due to chronic medical, cognitive, or mental health conditions and/or disabilities. The  

[7:3:66,318] Department certifies CBAS centers for participation in the Medi-Cal Program. Under a 1915(c) Medicaid home and community-

[7:4:66,328] based services waiver, MSSP provides health and social care management to prevent premature and unnecessary long-term  

[7:5:66,338] care institutionalization of frail adults aged 65 or older who otherwise would be placed in a nursing facility.

[5:0:66,218] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,237] 3930 - COMMISSION ON AGING  

[6:0:66,255] The Commission's statutory responsibilities include assisting with the development of the Department of Aging's State Plan on  

[6:1:66,265] Aging, monitoring the plan's progress and information-gathering. As part of its information-gathering responsibility, the  

[6:2:66,275] Commission holds meetings and public hearings around the state to address the issues and concerns of older Californians,  

[6:3:66,285] their families and caregivers. The Commission uses information gathered from these meetings and public hearings to develop  

[6:4:66,295] initiatives and projects focused on older Californians and their caregivers. The Commission holds membership on the  

[6:5:66,306] Governor’s Master Plan for Aging Stakeholder Advisory Committee and Taskforce for Alzheimer’s Disease Prevention and  

[6:6:66,316] Preparedness and provides administrative support for the Area Agency on Aging Advisory Councils of California. The Area  

[6:7:66,326] Agency on Aging Advisory Councils of California is supported solely by voluntary contributions made through a check-off box  

[6:8:66,336] included on the state income tax form.

[8:0:169,311] 4260   State Department of Health Care Services  

[9:0:66,333] The mission of the State Department of Health Care Services (DHCS) is to provide Californians with access to affordable,  

[9:1:66,343] integrated, high-quality health care, including medical, dental, mental health, substance use treatment services, and long-term  

[9:2:66,353] care. To fulfill its mission, DHCS finances and administers a number of individual health care service delivery programs,  

[9:3:66,363] including the state's Medicaid Program (Medi-Cal), which provides health care services to low-income persons and families  

[9:4:66,373] who meet defined eligibility requirements.

[11:0:66,342] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[12:0:65,361] • COVID-19 Medi-Cal Caseload Impacts—The Budget projects an average monthly caseload of 14.5 million beneficiaries in  

[12:1:76,371] 2021-22 and includes $9.4 billion ($2.5 billion General Fund) for increased caseload attributable to the federal continuous  

[12:2:76,382] coverage requirement related to the COVID-19 Public Health Emergency. Caseload is projected to peak at 14.8 million  

[12:3:76,392] beneficiaries in January 2022.  

[13:0:65,408] • Additional COVID-19 Impacts—The Budget includes net costs of $2.4 billion total funds ($2 billion General Fund savings) in  

[13:1:76,418] 2021-22 for COVID-19 impacts, including enhanced FMAP savings, testing in schools, vaccine administration costs, and  

[13:2:76,428] federal waiver flexibilities. The Budget assumes enhanced FMAP savings and flexibilities will remain in effect through the last  

[13:3:76,439] quarter of 2021.  

[14:0:65,455] • State-Only Claiming Adjustment—The Budget includes $180.4 million General Fund in 2021-22 for retroactive pharmacy  

[14:1:76,465] costs, as well as ongoing dental, pharmacy, managed care, Targeted Case Management, and behavioral health costs  

[14:2:76,475] associated with state-only populations.  

[15:0:65,491] • Medi-Cal Rx—The Budget includes costs of $362.6 million ($134.3 million General Fund) in 2021-22 associated with the  

[15:1:76,502] carve-out of the Medi-Cal pharmacy benefit from managed care to fee-for-service assumed January 1, 2022. Full annual  

[15:2:76,512] savings are projected to be approximately $859 million ($309.3 million General Fund) by 2023-24.  

[16:0:65,528] • Continuous Glucose Monitors Benefit—The Budget includes $4.9 million ($1.3 million General Fund) in 2021-22 to add  

[16:1:76,538] continuous glucose monitors as a covered Medi-Cal benefit for adult individuals with type 1 diabetes, effective January 1,  

[16:2:76,548] 2022. These costs are net of rebate savings for both adults and individuals aged 21 or younger.  

[17:0:65,565] • Telehealth Flexibilities—The Budget includes $151.1 million ($53.3 million General Fund) in 2021-22 for the extension of  

[17:1:76,575] telehealth flexibilities allowed during the federal public health emergency—including payment parity for audio-only  

[17:2:76,585] modalities—through December 2022 and coverage of remote patient monitoring, and includes $106.4 million ($37.2 million  

[17:3:76,595] General Fund) ongoing for remote patient monitoring.  

[18:0:65,611] • Elimination of Suspension of Medi-Cal Adult Optional Benefits—The Budget includes $47.0 million ($15.6 million General  

[18:1:76,622] Fund) in 2021-22 and $119.7 million ($39.7 million General Fund) ongoing to eliminate the suspension of certain adult  

[18:2:76,632] optional benefits, including podiatric services, audiology services, speech therapy, optician and optical services, and  

[18:3:76,642] incontinence creams and washes.  

[19:0:65,658] • County Administration—The Budget includes an increase of $50.3 million ($17.6 million General Fund) in 2021-22 for county  

[19:1:76,668] eligibility determination activities based on growth in the California Consumer Price Index. Additionally, the Budget includes  

[19:2:76,679] one-time funding of $73 million ($36.5 million General Fund) in each of 2021-22 and 2022-23 to resume annual Medi-Cal  

[19:3:76,689] redeterminations upon conclusion of the federal public health emergency and continuous coverage requirement.

[4:0:65,105] • California Advancing and Innovating Medi-Cal (CalAIM) Initiative—The Budget includes $1.6 billion ($650.7 million General  

[4:1:76,115] Fund) in 2021-22 for the CalAIM initiative. The cost increases in General Fund to $1.5 billion ($812.5 million General Fund)  

[4:2:76,126] by 2023-24 and decreases to $900 million ($480 million General Fund) in 2024-25 and ongoing.  

[5:0:65,142] • Population Health Management Service—As part of the CalAIM initiative, the Budget includes one-time $315 million ($31.5  

[5:1:76,152] million General Fund) to provide population health management services that would centralize administrative and clinical  

[5:2:76,162] data from the Department, health plans, and providers. Access to this information would allow all parties to better identify  

[5:3:76,172] and stratify member risks and inform quality and value delivery across the continuum of care while implementing the CalAIM  

[5:4:76,183] Initiative. The service will also facilitate the connection between important health data and critical human service data for a  

[5:5:76,193] given beneficiary.  

[6:0:65,209] • Medi-Cal Providing Access and Transforming Health Payments (PATH)—As part of the CalAIM Initiative, the Budget includes  

[6:1:76,219] one-time $200 million ($100 million General Fund) to build capacity for effective pre-release care for justice-involved  

[6:2:76,229] populations to enable coordination with justice agencies and Medi-Cal coverage of services 30 days prior to release.  

[7:0:65,246] • Five-Year Medi-Cal Eligibility Extension for Postpartum Individuals—The American Rescue Plan Act of 2021 allows states to  

[7:1:76,256] receive a federal funding match if they extend Medi-Cal eligibility from 60 days to 12 months for postpartum individuals,  

[7:2:76,266] effective April 1, 2022 for up to five years. The Budget includes $90.5 million ($45.3 million General Fund) in 2021-22 and  

[7:3:76,276] $362.2 million ($181.1 million General Fund) in 2022-23 growing to approximately $400 million ($200 million General Fund)  

[7:4:76,286] until April 1, 2027 to implement the extension.  

[8:0:65,303] • Doula Benefit—The Budget includes $403,000 ($152,000 General Fund) in 2021-22 and approximately $4.4 million ($1.7  

[8:1:76,313] million General Fund) annually at full implementation to add doula services as a covered benefit in the Medi-Cal program,  

[8:2:76,323] effective January 1, 2022.  

[9:0:65,339] • Community Health Workers—The Budget includes $16.3 million ($6.2 million General Fund), increasing to $201 million ($76  

[9:1:76,349] million General Fund) by 2026-27, to allow community health workers to provide benefits and services to Medi-Cal  

[9:2:76,360] beneficiaries, effective January 1, 2022.  

[10:0:65,376] • Medically Tailored Meals—The Budget includes one-time funding of $9.3 million General Fund to continue the provision of  

[10:1:76,386] medically tailored meals in the period between the conclusion of the existing pilot program in 2021 and when medically  

[10:2:76,396] tailored meals become available as an option for In-Lieu of Service under CalAIM.  

[11:0:65,412] • Proposition 56 Suspensions—The Budget eliminates suspensions for Proposition 56 supplemental payment increases.  

[12:0:65,429] • The Children and Youth Behavioral Health Initiative—The Budget includes $4.6 billion ($3.4 billion General Fund) over five  

[12:1:76,439] years, to transform California’s behavioral health system for children and youth into an innovative and prevention-focused  

[12:2:76,449] system where all children and youth are routinely screened, supported, and served for emerging and existing behavioral  

[12:3:76,459] health needs regardless of payer. Of this funding, $3.4 billion ($2.4 billion General Fund), including $255 million from the  

[12:4:76,469] Behavioral Health Continuum Infrastructure Program, is available for the Department of Health Care Services to implement  

[12:5:76,480] specified components of the Initiative.  

[13:0:65,496] • Behavioral Health Continuum Infrastructure Program—The Budget includes $2.2 billion ($1.7 billion General Fund) over  

[13:1:76,506] three years, for competitive grants to qualified entities to construct, acquire, and rehabilitate real estate assets or to invest in  

[13:2:76,516] mobile crisis infrastructure to expand the community continuum of behavioral health treatment resources.

[14:2:76,553] effective July 1, 2021.

[15:2:76,589] upon conclusion of the federal public health emergency and continuous coverage requirement.  

[16:0:65,606] • Support for Public Hospitals and Health Systems—The Budget includes $300 million one-time General Fund to help public  

[16:1:76,616] health care systems cover costs associated with critical care delivery needs provided during and beyond the pandemic.  

[17:0:65,632] • Rates for Intermediate Care Facilities for Individuals with Developmental Disabilities (ICF/DD) and Freestanding Pediatric  

[17:1:76,642] Subacute Facilities (FS/PSA)—The Budget includes $45.4 million ($21.6 million General Fund) in 2021-22 and $22 million

[3:0:66,69] Medi-Cal is responsible for coordinating and directing the delivery of health care services to low-income Californians. Medi-Cal  

[3:1:66,79] provides access to high-quality health care services in a fiscally prudent manner through both fee-for-service and managed  

[3:2:66,89] care delivery systems. Medi-Cal activities are carried out through the following functional groups: Health Care Benefits and  

[3:3:66,99] Eligibility; Health Care Delivery Systems; Health Care Financing; Behavioral Health; Audits and Investigations; Enterprise Data  

[3:4:66,109] and Information Management; and Program Operations.  

[3:5:66,127] 3960023 - CHILDREN'S MEDICAL SERVICES  

[4:0:66,145] Children's Medical Services is responsible for coordinating and directing the delivery of health care services to low-income and  

[4:1:66,155] seriously ill children, including the Child Health and Disability Prevention Program, the Genetically Handicapped Persons  

[4:2:66,165] Program, and the California Children's Services Program. These services are provided through Health Care Delivery Systems’  

[4:3:66,175] Integrated Systems of Care Division.  

[5:0:66,193] 3960032 - PRIMARY, RURAL, AND INDIAN HEALTH  

[6:0:66,211] Primary, Rural, and Indian Health is responsible for coordinating and directing the delivery of health care to Californians in rural  

[6:1:66,221] areas and to underserved populations through the following programs: Indian Health Program, American Indian Infant Health  

[6:2:66,231] Initiative, Rural Health Services Development Program, Seasonal Agricultural and Migratory Workers Program, State Office of  

[6:3:66,241] Rural Health, Medicare Rural Hospital Flexibility Program, Small Rural Hospital Improvement Grant Program, Tribal Emergency  

[6:4:66,252] Preparedness Program, and the J1 Visa Program.  

[6:5:66,269] 3960050 - OTHER CARE SERVICES  

[7:0:66,290] The Department is responsible for coordinating and directing the delivery of non Medi-Cal community mental health services  

[7:1:66,300] and substance use   treatment services: cancer screening services to low-income, under-insured, or uninsured individuals; and  

[7:2:66,310] prostate cancer treatment services to low-income, under-insured, or uninsured men. These services are provided through  

[7:3:66,320] the   Behavioral Health Community   Services Division, Behavioral Health Licensing and Certification Division, the Every Woman  

[7:4:66,331] Counts Program, and the Prostate Cancer Treatment Program.  

[7:5:66,348] 9900100 - DEPARTMENTAL ADMINISTRATION  

[8:0:66,366] This program provides overall organizational management, planning, policy development, and support for all DHCS programs.  

[8:1:66,376] This program is carried out by the Director’s Office, Administration, the Office of Administrative Hearings and Appeals, the  

[8:2:66,386] Office of Communications, Enterprise Technology Services, Fiscal, Legislative and Governmental Affairs, the Office of Legal  

[8:3:66,397] Services, and the Office of Civil Rights.

[6:0:207,363] 4265   Department of Public Health  

[7:0:66,385] The California Department of Public Health (Public Health) is dedicated to optimizing the health and well-being of all  

[7:1:66,395] Californians through the following core activities:  

[7:2:65,413] • Protecting the public from communicable diseases.  

[7:3:65,429] • Protecting the public from unhealthy and unsafe environments, and improving social determinants of health and healthy  

[7:4:76,439] communities.  

[8:0:65,455] • Preventing disease, disability, and premature death; and reducing or eliminating health disparities by embedding health and  

[8:1:76,466] mental health equity language, tools, and approaches into all public health and partner agency policies, programs, systems,  

[8:2:76,476] and resource allocation.  

[8:3:65,492] • Preparing for and responding to public health emergencies.  

[8:4:65,508] • Producing and disseminating data to evaluate population health status; inform people, institutions and communities; and to  

[8:5:76,518] guide public health strategies, programs, and actions.  

[8:6:65,535] • Promoting healthy lifestyles for individuals and families in their communities and workplaces.  

[8:7:65,551] • Providing access to quality, population-based health services.  

[8:8:66,568] Because Public Health's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay program to  

[8:9:66,579] support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[2:0:172,32] 4265   Department of Public Health - Continued 

[3:0:66,88] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[4:0:66,107] Children and Youth Behavioral Health Initiative: Public Education and Change Campaign —The Budget includes $100  

[4:1:66,118] million over five years to support a public education campaign, to be coordinated by the Public Health Office of Health Equity,  

[4:2:66,128] with a goal of raising the behavioral health literacy of all Californians and to normalize and support the prevention and early  

[4:3:66,138] intervention of mental health and substance use challenges.  

[5:0:66,156] California Reducing Disparities Project —The Budget includes a $63.1 million one-time General Fund investment to scale-up  

[5:1:66,166] the Project’s activities throughout the state. The Project supports pilot projects operated by community-based organizations,  

[5:2:66,176] which provide culturally responsive mental health and behavioral health services to historically underserved communities.  

[6:0:66,194] Sexually Transmitted Infection Interventions— The Budget includes ongoing $13 million General Fund to support  

[6:1:66,204] investments to end the epidemics of HIV/AIDS, Hepatitis C, and sexually transmitted infections.  

[7:0:66,222] California Parkinson’s and other Neurological Diseases Registry— The Budget includes $8.4 million and trailer bill  

[7:1:66,232] language to extend the sunset date of the California Parkinson's Disease Registry, as authorized by Chapter 103, Statutes of  

[7:2:66,242] 2020 (AB 2821), and to expand the types of neurological diseases tracked by the Registry.  

[8:0:66,260] Office of Suicide Prevention —The Budget includes ongoing $2.8 million General Fund to establish the Office of Suicide  

[8:1:66,270] Prevention within Public Health, as authorized by Chapter 142, Statutes of 2020 (AB 2112). The Office will consult with the  

[8:2:66,280] Mental Health Services Oversight and Accountability Commission (MHSOAC) to implement suicide prevention efforts in the  

[8:3:66,290] most vulnerable demographic groups, consistent with the MHSOAC’s “Striving for Zero” suicide prevention report.  

[9:0:66,308] Pandemic Response Review— The Budget includes a $3 million one-time General Fund investment to assess the state’s  

[9:1:66,318] COVID-19 public health response, evaluate lessons learned, and identify programmatic needs that will be used to inform future  

[9:2:66,328] action.  

[10:0:66,346] COVID-19 Direct Response Expenditures— The Budget includes a total of $1.7 billion for various state departments for direct  

[10:1:66,356] response costs associated with responding to and mitigating the impacts of the COVID-19 Pandemic in 2021-22. Specifically,  

[10:2:66,367] the budget includes $1.08 billion for the Department of Public Health to fund COVID-19 response costs. These funds will  

[10:3:66,377] support testing and laboratory operations, vaccination, medical surge capacity, contact tracing management, and other state  

[10:4:66,387] operations needs.

[25:0:66,621] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[26:0:66,640] 4040010 - Emergency Preparedness  

[27:0:66,658] The Public Health Emergency Preparedness program coordinates preparedness, response, and recovery efforts for all public  

[27:1:66,668] health emergencies, including natural disasters, acts of terrorism, and pandemic diseases. The program plans and supports  

[27:2:66,678] surge capacity in the medical care and public health systems to meet the needs of the state during emergencies. The program  

[27:3:66,689] also administers federal and state funds to support Public Health emergency preparedness and response activities.  

[28:0:66,706] 4045 - PUBLIC AND ENVIRONMENTAL HEALTH

[0:0:66,13] HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES 

[1:0:412,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — HHS   97 

[2:0:172,32] 4265   Department of Public Health - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The Public and Environmental Health programs provide public health services of: communicable disease control; chronic  

[3:1:66,79] disease and injury prevention; environmental public health; maternal, child, and family health; and vital records. These  

[3:2:66,89] programs function as part of the greater public health system throughout California.  

[4:0:66,107] 4045010 - Healthy Communities  

[5:0:66,124] This program works to prevent and control chronic diseases such as cancer, cardiovascular diseases, asthma, and diabetes; to  

[5:1:66,135] reduce the prevalence of obesity; to reduce tobacco use; to improve oral health through prevention, education, and community  

[5:2:66,145] efforts; to provide training programs for the public health workforce; to prevent and control injuries, violence, deaths, and  

[5:3:66,155] diseases related to behavioral, environmental, and occupational factors; to promote and support safe and healthy environments  

[5:4:66,165] in communities and workplaces; and to prevent substance and gambling addiction and treat gambling disorder. This program  

[5:5:66,175] includes the California Tobacco Control Branch, Childhood Lead Poisoning Prevention Branch, Chronic Disease Control  

[5:6:66,186] Branch, Chronic Disease Surveillance and Research Branch, Environmental Health Laboratory Branch, Environmental Health  

[5:7:66,196] Investigations Branch, Nutrition Education and Obesity Prevention Branch, Occupational Health Branch, Injury and Violence  

[5:8:66,206] Prevention Branch, Substance and Addiction Prevention Branch, and the Office of Oral Health.  

[6:0:66,224] 4045023 - Infectious Diseases  

[7:0:66,241] This program works to prevent and control infectious diseases such as: HIV/AIDS, viral hepatitis, influenza and other vaccine-

[7:1:66,252] preventable illnesses, sexually transmitted diseases, tuberculosis, emerging infections, and foodborne illnesses. This program  

[7:2:66,262] includes the Division of Communicable Disease Control, the Office of AIDS, the Office of Binational Border Health, and the  

[7:3:66,272] Office of Refugee Health.  

[8:0:66,290] 4045032 - Family Health  

[9:0:66,307] This program works to improve and reduce disparities in health outcomes for girls and women of reproductive age, pregnant  

[9:1:66,318] and postpartum women, infants, children, adolescents, and their families. This program includes Genetic Disease Screening;  

[9:2:66,328] Maternal, Child, and Adolescent Health; and the Special Supplemental Nutrition Program for Women, Infants, and Children.  

[10:0:66,346] 4045041 - Health Statistics and Informatics  

[11:0:66,363] This program works to improve the public’s health by managing information systems and facilitating the collection, validation,  

[11:1:66,373] analysis, and dissemination of health statistics and demographic information on the California population. This program  

[11:2:66,384] includes Vital Records and Public Health Informatics.  

[12:0:66,401] 4045050 - County Health Services  

[13:0:66,419] This program supports county-based public health information and services, including the Medical Marijuana Identification Card  

[13:1:66,429] Program.  

[14:0:66,447] 4045059 - Environmental Health  

[15:0:66,465] This program works to protect and improve the health of all California residents by providing for the safety of food, drugs, and  

[15:1:66,475] medical devices; conducting underage tobacco enforcement; overseeing the use of radiation and radioactive materials;  

[15:2:66,485] regulating the disposal and handling of medical waste; and conducting other environmental management programs. This  

[15:3:66,495] program includes Environmental Management, Radiologic Health, Drinking Water and Radiation Laboratory, Food and Drug  

[15:4:66,505] Safety, and the Food and Drug Laboratory.  

[16:0:66,523] 4050 - LICENSING AND CERTIFICATION  

[17:0:66,541] 4050010 - Health Facilities  

[18:0:66,559] This program regulates the quality of care in over 11,000 public and private health facilities, clinics, and agencies throughout  

[18:1:66,569] the state; licenses nursing home administrators; certifies nurse assistants, home health aides, and hemodialysis technicians;  

[18:2:66,579] and oversees the prevention, surveillance and reporting of healthcare-associated infections in California's general acute care  

[18:3:66,589] hospitals.  

[19:0:66,607] 4050019 - Laboratory Field Services  

[20:0:66,625] This program regulates California laboratory, blood bank, biologics, and tissue bank quality standards through licensure and  

[20:1:66,635] oversight of approximately 22,000 clinical laboratories, public health laboratories, blood banks, biologics facilities, and tissue  

[20:2:66,645] banks in California; and approximately 60,000 laboratory personnel in more than 30 different categories of laboratory personnel  

[20:3:66,655] including cytotechnologists, medical laboratory technicians, phlebotomists, clinical laboratory scientists, and public health  

[20:4:66,665] microbiologists.  

[21:0:66,683] 9900 - DEPARTMENTAL ADMINISTRATION  

[22:0:66,701] This program provides overall management, planning, policy development, and administrative support services for all Public  

[22:1:66,711] Health programs. This program is carried out by the Executive Division, the Office of Health Equity, the Office of Compliance,  

[23:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[7:0:66,369] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[7:1:66,388] The Department of Public Health operates a single laboratory facility in Richmond, California. The laboratory provides  

[7:2:66,399] analytical, diagnostic, developmental, evaluative, epidemiological, reference, quality control, educational, training, and  

[7:3:66,409] consultative laboratory services related to the protection and promotion of public health. The Richmond campus consists of  

[7:4:66,419] 697,000 gross square feet of laboratory, office, and support facilities on 29 acres.

[4:0:176,140] 4300   Department of Developmental Services  

[5:0:66,162] The Department of Developmental Services is responsible under the Lanterman Developmental Disabilities Services Act for  

[5:1:66,172] providing persons with developmental disabilities the services and supports they need to lead more independent and  

[5:2:66,182] productive lives and to make choices and decisions about their lives. The Department sets broad policy and provides  

[5:3:66,192] leadership for developmental services statewide; establishes priorities, standards, and procedures within which the  

[6:0:66,203] developmental services program operates; monitors, reviews, and evaluates service delivery; and helps to remediate problems  

[6:1:66,213] that arise. Services are delivered through a statewide network of 21 private, nonprofit, locally based community agencies  

[6:2:66,223] known as regional centers as well as through state-operated facilities. The Department, through the 21 regional centers,  

[6:3:66,233] oversees coordination of services to persons with developmental disabilities; provides that such services are planned,  

[6:4:66,243] provided, and meet the needs and choices of these individuals at each stage of their lives; and, to the extent possible,  

[6:5:66,254] accomplishes these goals in the individual's home community.  

[7:0:66,271] Because the Department of Developmental Services' programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has  

[7:1:66,282] a capital outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure  

[7:2:66,292] Overview."

[6:0:66,157] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[7:0:65,176] • Service Provider Rate Reform—The Budget includes $146 million ($89.9 million General Fund) in 2021-22, growing to $2.1  

[7:1:76,186] billion ($1.2 billion General Fund) ongoing in 2025-26, for provider rate increases based on the DDS 2019 Rate Study.  

[7:2:76,196] Annual funding includes resources for development and implementation of a quality incentive payment program focused on  

[7:3:76,207] improving consumer outcomes and service quality.  

[8:0:65,223] • COVID-19 Direct Response Expenditures — The Budget includes a total of $1.7 billion for various state departments for direct  

[8:1:76,233] response costs associated with responding to and mitigating the impacts of the COVID-19 Pandemic in 2021-22.  

[8:2:76,243] Specifically, the budget includes $15 million General Fund for the Department of Developmental Services to develop surge  

[8:3:76,253] sites to serve consumers diagnosed with, exposed to, or at high risk of COVID-19. This level of funding will support an  

[8:4:76,264] average of 20 beds at the Fairview Developmental Center and 10 beds at the Porterville Developmental Center through  

[8:5:76,274] December 31, 2021.  

[9:0:65,290] • Elimination of Suspensions—The Budget eliminates suspension of the Provider Supplemental Rate Increase and repeals the  

[9:1:76,300] Uniform Holiday Schedule resulting in a cost of $527.6 million ($309.6 million General Fund) ongoing.  

[10:0:65,316] • Performance Incentives Program—The Budget includes $5.6 million ($4 million General Fund) in 2021-22 for one-time  

[10:1:76,327] planning resources to create an outcome-focused regional center operations funding program. Beginning in 2022-23,  

[10:2:76,337] ongoing costs increase to $89.4 million ($61 million General Fund) to reduce caseload ratios, provide funds for targeted  

[10:3:76,347] staffing to improve consumer and family experiences, and implement the performance incentives program.  

[11:0:65,363] • Direct Service Professional Workforce Training and Development—The Budget includes $4.3 million ($2.9 million General  

[11:1:76,373] Fund) in 2021-22 to establish a training and certification program for direct service professionals tied to wage differentials.

[5:0:66,615] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,634] 4140 - COMMUNITY SERVICES PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,655] Through the network of regional centers, the Department supports the development and maintenance of services for eligible  

[6:1:66,665] persons with developmental disabilities who reside in the community. The regional centers directly provide or coordinate the  

[6:2:66,675] following services and supports: (1) information and referral, (2) assessment and diagnosis, (3) counseling, (4) lifelong  

[6:3:66,685] individualized planning and service coordination, (5) purchase of necessary services included in the individual program plan,  

[6:4:66,696] (6) assistance in finding and using community and other resources, (7) advocacy for the protection of legal, civil, and service  

[6:5:66,706] rights, (8) early intervention services for infants and their families, (9) family support, (10) planning, placement, and monitoring  

[7:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — HHS   142 

[1:0:419,13] HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES 

[2:0:141,32] 4300   Department of Developmental Services - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] for 24-hour out-of-home care, (11) training and educational opportunities for individuals and families, (12) community education  

[3:1:66,79] about developmental disabilities, and (13) habilitation services.  

[4:0:66,97] 4145 - STATE-OPERATED RESIDENTIAL AND COMMUNITY FACILITIES PROGRAM  

[5:0:66,114] The Department currently operates two residential facilities: Fairview Developmental Center (Orange County) and Porterville  

[5:1:66,124] Developmental Center (Tulare County). Secure treatment services are provided at the Porterville Developmental Center  

[5:2:66,135] for   individuals who have been found incompetent to stand trial due to their developmental disability. The Department is  

[5:3:66,145] projected to operate seven Stabilization, Training, Assistance, and Reintegration (STAR) residences in Northern, Central, and  

[5:4:66,155] Southern California   to provide acute crisis services in the community.   The Department also leases one small community facility  

[5:5:66,165] for persons who require specialized behavioral interventions: Canyon Springs, a 63-bed facility in Cathedral City (Riverside  

[5:6:66,175] County).  

[6:0:66,193] All   residential facilities are licensed as General Acute Care Hospitals with distinct parts for skilled nursing care and intermediate  

[6:1:66,203] care. Canyon Springs operates as an intermediate care facility, and STAR residences operate as community crisis homes.  

[6:2:66,214] Services at all facilities involve the provision of active treatment through residential and day programs on a 24-hour basis,  

[6:3:66,224] including appropriate medical and dental care, health maintenance activities, and assistance with activities of daily living,  

[6:4:66,234] training, education, and employment.  

[7:0:66,252] Individuals served in the STAR crisis   residences are committed by a court order to the Department for suitable treatment and  

[7:1:66,262] habilitation services during a crisis and are provided services and supports necessary to prepare them for return to their  

[7:2:66,272] residential home or to transition to a new living option. The primary objective of this program   is to provide a safety net of  

[7:3:66,282] services that include providing care, treatment, crisis services, and habilitation services to residents based upon assessed  

[7:4:66,292] need and as outlined in each person's Individual Program Plan. These services are offered in the most efficient, effective, and  

[7:5:66,303] least restrictive manner to all individuals referred by the regional centers and/or the judicial system; and are designed to teach  

[7:6:66,313] individuals the skills for increased independence, provide for preservation or improvement of health and welfare, and enhance  

[7:7:66,323] personal competence in all areas of daily living.  

[8:0:66,341] Fairview Developmental Center and the non-secure treatment portion of Porterville Developmental Center transitioned the last  

[8:1:66,351] resident into a community setting in February 2020.  

[9:0:66,369] 4149 - PROGRAM ADMINISTRATION  

[10:0:66,389] The objective of this program is to: (1) provide overall management, planning and policy development, legal, legislative, audit,  

[10:1:66,399] and administrative services; (2) manage revenue and reimbursement collections from federal and state programs, insurance  

[10:2:66,409] companies, and private payers for the cost of services; (3) monitor regional center operations for compliance with statute,  

[10:3:66,420] regulations, and their contract with the Department; (4) provide central administrative and clinical management services to  

[10:4:66,430] the   state-operated residential (developmental center) and community   facilities to oversee the quality of services provided,  

[10:5:66,440] compliance with state licensing and federal certification requirements, protection of consumers and staff, and maintenance of  

[10:6:66,450] facility structures and grounds; and (5) oversee areas that include the development of policies and procedures for  

[10:7:66,460] developmental center operations, law enforcement and protective services, facility population management, program and fiscal  

[10:8:66,471] oversight, and facility planning and support.

[9:0:66,531] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[9:1:66,550] The Department of Developmental Services is responsible for the operation and maintenance of the facilities under its control,  

[9:2:66,560] including two state-owned and operated 24-hour care facilities and related buildings, grounds, and infrastructure. The facilities  

[9:3:66,571] are Fairview Developmental Center in Orange County and Porterville Developmental Center in Tulare County.  

[9:4:66,588] The developmental centers are comprised of approximately 2.3 million gross square feet in 319   buildings on 784 acres.   As of  

[9:5:66,599] February 2020, the Fairview Developmental Center and the Porterville General Treatment Area transitioned all former residents  

[9:6:66,609] to community living.   The Department will continue to maintain the Fairview property in warm shutdown mode until disposition  

[9:7:66,619] of the property.   The Department is projected to operate seven Stabilization, Training, Assistance, and Reintegration (STAR)  

[9:8:66,629] programs that provide acute crisis services in the community.   Additionally, the Department is responsible for the maintenance  

[9:9:66,639] of interior finishes and equipment at Canyon Springs,   a 57,000-square-foot leased facility in Riverside County.   These facilities  

[9:10:66,650] are used to aid the Department's mission   in providing medical, dental, and nursing care; active treatment; supervision;  

[9:11:66,660] education; and vocational training for residents with developmental disabilities.  

[10:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[10:0:201,469] 4440   Department of State Hospitals  

[11:0:66,490] The Department of State Hospitals manages the nation's largest inpatient forensic mental health hospital system. Its mission is  

[11:1:66,501] to provide evaluation and treatment in a safe and responsible manner, seeking innovation and excellence in state hospital  

[11:2:66,511] operations, across a continuum of care. The Department is responsible for the daily care and provision of mental health  

[11:3:66,521] treatment for its patients in five state hospitals located in Atascadero, Coalinga, Metropolitan-Los Angeles, Napa, and  

[11:4:66,531] Patton,   and employs over 13,000 staff. Additionally, the Department provides services in jail-based competency treatment  

[11:5:66,541] programs and conditional release programs throughout the 58 counties. In 2019-20, the Department served 10,962 patients  

[11:6:66,552] within state hospitals and jail-based facilities, with average daily censuses of 6,143 and   333 respectively. The conditional  

[11:7:66,562] release program   maintains an average daily census of approximately 650.  

[12:0:66,580] Because the Department of State Hospitals's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital  

[12:1:66,590] outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure  

[12:2:66,600] Overview."

[7:3:76,481] acute bed capacity, including, but not limited to, facilities such as Institutions for Mental Disease, Mental Health

[3:0:66,69] functions such as: policy development and management, licensing oversight, patients' rights coordination, clinical oversight,

[5:0:66,124] 4400010 - HEADQUARTERS ADMINISTRATION  

[6:0:66,142] Department of State Hospitals Headquarters oversees the California state hospital system, which provides mental health  

[6:1:66,152] services to patients admitted into   its facilities. Headquarters Administration includes headquarters functions such as: policy  

[6:2:66,163] development and management, licensing oversight, patients' rights coordination, clinical oversight, data research, fiscal,  

[6:3:66,173] personnel and contracts management, as well as legal services.  

[7:0:66,190] 4400020 - HOSPITAL ADMINISTRATION  

[8:0:66,208] Department of State Hospitals Headquarters includes a Hospital Administration component. Hospital Administration includes  

[8:1:66,218] centralized functions and services that directly affect patient care but are administered centrally from headquarters such as:  

[8:2:66,229] technology services, hospital police and fire oversight, patient referral management, clinical recruitment, patient benefit and  

[8:3:66,239] billing management, and Medicare revenue collection.  

[9:0:66,256] 4410 - STATE HOSPITALS  

[10:0:66,274] The state hospital system includes five state hospitals located at Atascadero, Metropolitan-Los Angeles, Napa, Patton, and  

[10:1:66,284] Coalinga. Mental health treatment services at all facilities are delivered by clinical teams who provide full-time inpatient care to  

[10:2:66,295] those with mental illness and those incapable of living in the community. The state hospitals treat the following types of  

[10:3:66,305] patients: civil commitments under the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act (LPS); forensic commitments such as Incompetent to Stand  

[10:4:66,315] Trial (IST), Not Guilty by Reason of Insanity (NGI), Sexually Violent Predators (SVP), and Offender with a Mental Health  

[10:5:66,325] Disorder (OMD); and prisoners with mental illness ( Coleman ) from CDCR.  

[11:0:66,343] 4410010 - ATASCADERO  

[12:0:66,361] The Department of State Hospitals-Atascadero opened in 1954 and is located on the Central Coast of California in Atascadero  

[12:1:66,371] (San Luis Obispo County). The hospital is a self-contained psychiatric hospital constructed within a security perimeter. The  

[12:2:66,381] majority of the all-male patient population is remanded for treatment by   Superior Courts or by CDCR pursuant to various  

[12:3:66,391] sections of the California Penal Code   and the Welfare and Institutions Code.  

[13:0:66,409] DSH-Atascadero primarily serves the following four patient types: OMD,  Coleman   patients from CDCR, IST, and   NGI.  

[14:0:66,427] 4410020 - COALINGA  

[15:0:66,444] The Department of State Hospitals-Coalinga opened in 2005 and is located on the western edge of Fresno County. The  

[15:1:66,454] hospital is California’s newest forensic mental health hospital and was created to primarily treat SVPs. It is a self-contained  

[15:2:66,465] psychiatric hospital constructed with a security perimeter. CDCR provides perimeter security as well as transportation of  

[15:3:66,475] patients to outside medical services and court proceedings. The majority of the all-male patient population is remanded for  

[15:4:66,485] treatment by   Superior Courts or CDCR pursuant to various sections of the California Penal Code and the Welfare and  

[15:5:66,495] Institutions Code.  

[16:0:66,513] DSH-Coalinga primarily serves SVP, OMD and  Coleman   patients from CDCR.  

[17:0:66,531] 4410030 - METROPOLITAN  

[18:0:66,548] The Department of State Hospitals-Metropolitan opened in 1916 and is located in Norwalk (Los Angeles County). The hospital  

[18:1:66,559] is an open style campus within a security perimeter. Due to concerns raised by the community, DSH-Metropolitan maintains a  

[18:2:66,569] formal agreement with the City of Norwalk and the Los Angeles County Sheriff not to accept patients charged with murder or a  

[18:3:66,579] sex crime, or that are at high risk for escape.  

[19:0:66,597] DSH-Metropolitan primarily serves the following four patient types: LPS, IST, OMD, and NGI.  

[20:0:66,614] 4410040 - NAPA  

[21:0:66,632] The Department of State Hospitals-Napa opened in 1875 and is located in Napa County. The hospital   was the first of the  

[21:1:66,642] five   State Hospitals, and is   the oldest California state hospital still in operation.   It has an open style campus with a security  

[21:2:66,652] perimeter.  

[22:0:66,670] DSH-Napa primarily serves the following four patient types: LPS, IST, OMD, and NGI.  

[23:0:66,688] 4410050 - PATTON

[0:0:66,13] 2021-22 STATE BUDGET — HHS   154 

[1:0:419,13] HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES 

[2:0:166,32] 4440   Department of State Hospitals - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The Department of State Hospitals-Patton opened in 1893 and is located in San Bernardino County. The hospital has an open  

[3:1:66,79] style campus with a security perimeter. CDCR correctional officers provide perimeter security and transportation at DSH-Patton  

[3:2:66,89] as well as transportation of patients to outside medical services and some court proceedings.  

[4:0:66,107] DSH-Patton primarily serves the following five patient types: LPS, IST, OMD, NGI and female  Coleman   patients from CDCR.  

[5:0:66,124] 4410060 - HOSPITAL POLICE OFFICER ACADEMY  

[6:0:66,142] The Department of State Hospitals Police Officer (HPO) Academy is overseen by the Office of Protective Services, which is a  

[6:1:66,152] full service law enforcement agency that protects the safety and security of the state hospital facilities, staff and patients. DSH  

[6:2:66,163] provides training standards, model programs, cultural competency, program development, and a comprehensive training plan  

[6:3:66,173] to all protective services staff. The HPO Academy requires that cadets successfully complete 548 hours of multidisciplinary  

[6:4:66,183] training specific to the DSH police officer job specifications.  

[7:0:66,201] 4420 - CONDITIONAL RELEASE PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,221] The Conditional Release Program is the statewide system of community-based services for specified forensic patients.  

[8:1:66,231] Mandated as a state responsibility by the Governor's Mental Health Initiative of 1984, the program began operations on  

[8:2:66,241] January 1, 1986.  

[9:0:66,259] 4420010 - CONDITIONAL RELEASE PROGRAM  

[10:0:66,277] The Conditional Release Program General/Non-Sexually Violent Predators is comprised of   NGI, OMD, IST and SVP.  

[10:1:66,287] Individuals suitable for this program may be recommended by the state hospital medical director to the courts for outpatient  

[10:2:66,297] treatment. Currently, DSH contracts with seven county-operated and three private organizations   serving all 58 counties in the  

[10:3:66,307] state with non-SVP commitments.  

[11:0:66,325] 4420020 - CONDITIONAL RELEASE PROGRAM -   SEXUALLY VIOLENT PREDATORS  

[12:0:66,343] The Conditional Release Program-Sexually Violent Predators was added to the Conditional Release Program effective January  

[12:1:66,353] 1, 1996 per Welfare and Institutions Code 6604. Prior to the conditional release of the first   SVP in 2003,   conditional release  

[12:2:66,363] providers did not have treatment services that would allow them to accept sexually violent predators as patients, requiring the  

[12:3:66,373] Department of State Hospitals to enter into an annual contract with a single private provider serving 58 counties. Current statue  

[12:4:66,384] requires SVPs be conditionally released to their county of domicile and sufficient funding be available to provide treatment and  

[12:5:66,394] supervision services when an   SVP is conditionally released into the community by court order.  

[13:0:66,412] 4430 - CONTRACTED PATIENT SERVICES  

[14:0:66,432] The Department of State Hospitals contracts with local entities to provide mental health services to some of its patients outside  

[14:1:66,442] of state hospital facilities. This includes both the   Admissions, Evaluation, and Stabilization Center in the Kern County as well as  

[14:2:66,452] various other   jail-based competency treatment programs.  

[15:0:66,470] 4430010 - ADMISSION, EVALUATION, STABILIZATION CENTER 

[16:0:66,488] The Admissions, Evaluation, and Stabilization Center in the Kern County Jail receives   IST patients committed to the  

[16:1:66,498] Department of State Hospitals directly from catchment counties in southern California. Patients receive a full evaluation upon  

[16:2:66,508] admission to determine whether they can be treated through the Admissions, Evaluation, and Stabilization Center on a short-

[16:3:66,518] term basis or may need longer-term state hospital treatment. Patients considered to be short-term can be treated and  

[16:4:66,529] discharged directly from the Admissions, Evaluation, and Stabilization Center.  

[17:0:66,546] 4430020 - JAIL-BASED COMPETENCY TREATMENT  

[18:0:66,564] The Jail-Based Competency Treatment Program focuses on defendants deemed incompetent to stand trial and provides  

[18:1:66,574] mental health treatment to defendants in county jails, restoring them to competency, and allowing for participation in court  

[18:2:66,584] proceedings.  

[19:0:66,602] 4440 - EVALUATION AND FORENSIC SERVICES  

[20:0:66,620] Evaluation and Forensic Services is comprised of the   OMD and the Sex Offender Commitment Programs. Penal Code  

[20:1:66,630] Sections 2960-2981 require that a prisoner who meets specific criteria be ordered by the Board of Parole Hearings to be  

[20:2:66,640] treated by State Hospitals as a condition of parole. The Department of State Hospitals performs evaluations to determine if  

[20:3:66,650] individuals may meet the statutory criteria for the   OMD program. These evaluations are used by the Board of Parole Hearings  

[20:4:66,661] in determining whether a prisoner is committed to the Department of State Hospitals as a condition of parole. Welfare and  

[20:5:66,671] Institutions Code Section 6600 et seq. (Chapter 793, Statutes of 1995) was enacted January 1, 1996, establishing a new  

[20:6:66,681] category of civil commitment for persons found, upon release from prison, to be an SVP. The Sex Offender Commitment  

[20:7:66,691] Program administers clinical screenings and performs evaluations to determine if prisoners may meet the statutory criteria for  

[20:8:66,701] an SVP commitment to the Department of State Hospitals upon completion of their prison term. Superior Courts make the  

[21:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[6:0:66,363] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:66,382] 4170 - The Commission, established in 2004, provides oversight and accountability for portions of the Mental Health Services  

[7:1:66,392] Act (MHSA), Adult and Older Adult System of Care Act, Children’s Mental Health Services Act, and Mental Health Student  

[7:2:66,402] Services Act. The Commission’s primary roles include:  

[7:3:66,420] Program Review, Oversight and Accountability  

[8:0:66,438] The Commission provides oversight, review, training, technical assistance, accountability, and evaluation of specified mental  

[8:1:66,448] health projects and programs supported with MHSA funds.   This includes review and approval of county mental health  

[8:2:66,458] Innovation Programs   and Expenditure Plans.   It also includes assessing whether services that are provided pursuant to the  

[8:3:66,468] MHSA are cost-effective and in accordance with recommended best practices.  

[8:4:66,486] Policy Projects 

[9:0:66,504] The Commission may advise the Governor and the Legislature regarding actions the state may take to improve care and  

[9:1:66,514] services for   individuals living   with mental illness. The Commission executes projects designed to inform mental health policy by  

[9:2:66,524] integrating research findings and experiential knowledge. The Commission’s projects include criminal justice mental health, the  

[9:3:66,534] state suicide prevention plan, workplace mental health standards, prevention and early intervention strategies, and school-

[9:4:66,545] based mental health.  

[9:5:66,562] Strategic Partnerships  

[10:0:66,580] The Commission partners with universities, institutes, and public agencies to develop, field-test and implement changes and  

[10:1:66,590] policy solutions. The Commission’s partnerships include the Full Service Partnership Pilot, the Early Psychosis Learning Health  

[10:2:66,600] Care Network, the Youth Innovation Project, the Innovation Incubator project, and the suicide crisis center project.  

[10:3:66,618] Grant Programs  

[11:0:66,636] The Commission manages grant programs that incentivize stronger partnerships, integrated services, braided funding and the  

[11:1:66,646] evaluation required for continuous improvement. The Commission’s grant programs include the Mental Health Wellness Act of  

[11:2:66,656] 2013 triage personnel grants, youth drop-in centers, the early psychosis project, and the Mental Health Student Services Act. 

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:0:66,286] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,306] 4181 - ENERGY PROGRAMS  

[7:0:66,323] The Energy Programs assist low-income households in meeting their immediate and long-term home energy needs through  

[7:1:66,333] financial assistance, energy conservation, weatherization and renewable energy services.  

[8:0:66,351] The Low-Income Home Energy Assistance Program (LIHEAP) provides financial assistance to eligible low-income households  

[8:1:66,361] to offset the costs of heating and/or cooling residential dwellings, assistance for weather-related or energy-related  

[8:2:66,372] emergencies, and weatherization services to improve the energy efficiency of low-income residential dwellings and safeguard  

[8:3:66,382] the health and safety of household occupants. This program may include a leveraging incentive program in which  

[8:4:66,392] supplementary LIHEAP funds can be obtained by LIHEAP grantees if non-federal leveraged home energy resources are used  

[8:5:66,402] along with LIHEAP weatherization related services.  

[9:0:66,420] The Low Income Household Water Assistance Program (LIHWAP) is designed to provide financial assistance to low-income  

[9:1:66,430] households that pay a high proportion of household income for drinking water and wastewater services, by providing funds to  

[9:2:66,440] owners or operators of public water systems or treatment works to reduce arrearages of, and rates charged to, such  

[9:3:66,450] households for these services.  

[10:0:66,471] The Department of Energy Weatherization Assistance Program provides weatherization to improve the energy efficiency of low-

[10:1:66,481] income residential dwellings and safeguard the health and safety of household occupants.  

[11:0:66,499] The Low-Income Weatherization Program (LIWP) provides energy efficiency and renewable energy services in low-income  

[11:1:66,509] single-family and multi-family dwellings to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and lower energy costs. LIWP projects include  

[11:2:66,519] weatherization and solar photovoltaic systems installations.  

[12:0:66,537] The California Arrearage Payment Program (CAPP) is designed to prevent energy utility disconnections for households  

[12:1:66,547] experiencing financial hardship due to the economic impacts of the COVID-19 pandemic by providing financial assistance to  

[12:2:66,557] customer accounts in arrears.  

[13:0:66,593] 4185 - COMMUNITY SERVICES  

[14:0:66,610] The Community Services Block Grant (CSBG) is designed to enable local government and private nonprofit community  

[14:1:66,621] organizations to help low-income families achieve and maintain economic security through a broad range of activities. These  

[14:2:66,631] activities include education, employment services, emergency services, housing, income support and management, and health  

[14:3:66,641] and nutritional services. Additionally, CSBG funds are used by local community organizations to revitalize low-income  

[14:4:66,651] communities.  

[15:0:66,669] The California Earned Income Tax Credit (CalEITC) benefits low-income, working Californians by providing cash-back tax  

[15:1:66,679] credits to boost the income of eligible individuals and families. Since 2017-18,   the Department   has worked with the Franchise  

[15:2:66,689] Tax Board to provide grant opportunities for community-based organizations to increase awareness of CalEITC.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.
